PHILIPPINES
WATER RESOURCES DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
THE IRRIGATORS' ASSOCIATION (IA) DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM
I. BACKGROUND
1.    The concept of organizing farmers in groups was initiated in 1969 in response to the need
to secure better water management by introducing rotational irrigation by section of a farrn main
ditch. It required that farmers in a rotation area (about 50 acres) served by a tumout be organized
in farmers irrigators groups (FIGs).
2.    In 1980 the subsidies available to NIA were reduced. In order to improve its operating
efficiency and also to enhance the performance of the irrigation schemes, NIA undertook to
organize farmers with a view to involving them in the operations of the irrigation systems to
secure better water distribution, reduce operation and maintenance (O&M) costs and improve the
Irrigation Service Fees (ISF) collections.  To this end, NIA introduced the Participatory
Approach Program (PAP). Under the program, the farmers serviced by one turnout were
organized into Turnout Service Area Groups (TSA) -- former FIGs--which were affiliated with a
secondary level organization called the Irrigators Association (lAs). Under the concept each TSA
was expected to cover about 20 ha (about 15 farmers) and some 15 TSAs would be affiliated to
one IA which would serve, on average, about 250 farmers working on about 300 ha.
Organizational Set-up for Supporting lAs
3.    The overall responsibility for organizing and training lAs in NIA rests with the
Institutional Development Department (IDD), which is, at present, under the direct responsibility
of the Assistant Administrator, Systems Operation and the Equipment Management Sector
(SOEM).
4.    At the Head Office, IDD is organized in two Divisions, The Irrigators Organization
Division (IOD) and the Irrigators Assistance Division (IAD), the activities of which are
supervised by the Departmental Manager.
5.    The IOD, headed by a Division Manager, is responsible for Organization and Training. It
is staffed by a Division Manager, who is supported by three Irrigators Development Chiefs
(IDCs) who are respectively responsible for policy formulation and monitoring of NIS schemes,
CIS schemes, and registration of lAs. The IDCs are in turn supported by five Senior Irrigation
Development Officers (SIDOs). One SIDO is pursuing higher studies and the other four are
assigned to respectively monitor IOSP-II, registration of lAs with SEC, and the other projects
being implemented by NIA. In addition, there are two IDOs supporting the SIDOs in analyzing
and compiling data provided by the Regional Offices.



- 136 -
Annex 8
Page 2 of 16
6.    The IAD is staffed by a Division manager, two IDCs, six SIDOs and 1 Agriculturalist.
Of the two IDCs, one is away on a consulting assignment, and the other handles coordination
with other agencies for providing support services to the lAs. Of the six SIDOs, two are involved
in IMIS/IAMS training and another three are handling project Benefit Monitoring and
Evaluation. One SIDO has been seconded to the WRDP and the Agriculturalist has been
seconded to the IOSP-II Project Management Office.
7.    At the Regional Office, the overall responsibility for implementing the IDP rests with
IDD Division Managers, who are supported by SIDOs and IDOs responsible for supervision,
monitoring and training of staff and the lAs. In total, some 75 staff on the monthly payroll are
located in the 12 Regional Offices and UPRIIS and MARIIS, giving an average of 5.3
supervisory staff per Region/Major Irrigation Scheme.
8.    At the Field level NIA recruits IDOs to work with Water Masters (WRFTs) and Farmer
Irrigators Organizers (FIOS) to organize the lAs.
9.    The program was initiated by using degree holder Irrigation Community Organizers
(ICO), now referred to Institutional Development Officers (IDOs), to assist in the institutional
development of the lAs. As the program was conceptualized, the IDO was to be the principal
field worker to organize the farmers with the field level staffing varying with the type of schemes
being developed. In CIS, generally each IDO has one project, where he/she lives and another
project that he or she supervises part-time. On NIS the organizational arrangements were
conceptualized differently. The IDO was to be responsible for organizing, on average, 12.5 lAs
on 3,750 ha.
10.   At present, the practice varies with staff availability and local requirements. IDO
involvement is considered more critical in new projects rather than rehabilitation where the
farmers are considered to be familiar with irrigation and where, albeit inactive, an IA is perhaps
already organized. Because of this consideration and also to reduce costs, NIA trained the
Watermasters to organize the lAs with the support of FIOs identified by the WRFTs. The FIOs
are paid a stipend of P500/month to assist the lAs during the training and the organization phase,
after which the FIOs are expected to become ex-officio members, serving as Advisors (on
organizational issues) on a gratuity basis.
11.   The IDOs were employed as group organizers on a contractual basis with their contracts
terminating once the IAs were organized. Unfortunately, a number of them retarded the IA
development process to extend their tenure of employment, which led to some disillusionment
with their performance.
12.   Staffing. Besides the 250 IDOs working in the CIS schemes, there are a total of 885 staff
involved in NIS, the latter with varying degrees of commitment or time availability (see para 13).
Of these 17 are located at the Head office, 75 in the Regions including UPRIIS and MRIIS, and
the remaining 793 at the Field Offices (see Table 1). The Head Office and the Regional staff are
graduates with varying backgrounds (sociologists, agriculturalists, economists), while the
WRFTs have attained varying levels of educational qualifications. The older WRFTs have



- 137 -
Annex 8
Page 3 of 16
generally acquired school leaving certificates, while the younger employees may be graduates or
diploma holders. FIOs are farmers located at the schemes.
13.   Most of the NIS staff (779 out of 885 or 88%) are on the monthly payroll and, as part of
the regular civil service, are paid from the Corporate Operating Budget (COB). Of these, 687 or
88% are WRFTs who, in addition to being involved in IA development, also undertake to
coordinate water distribution, preparation of cropping plans and ISF collection. Their
involvement in IA development is only part-time, leaving only 92 regular staff participating in
the IDP on a full-time basis. Of the remaining 106 staff 100 are IDOs, paid from the projects on a
contractual basis and, as already noted, previous experience has indicated that IDOs without a
secure term of employment seem to undertake a results-oriented program without full
commitment. The FIOs are paid for only one year. In principle, it is expected that they will
continue to operate in advisory capacity once the IA is organized but only few continue to
operate in this role without compensation.
Procedures Followed in IA development
14.  The steps involved in the development of the lAs in NIS are as follows:
Step 1 Organizing the TSA groups, which discuss the operational and physical problems,
select the TSA leaders and discuss the draft of the constitution and by-laws that
will govern their IA. On being assigned to a village the IDO's first task is to
identify potential leaders through formal and informal discussions. Potential
leaders are appointed as officers (chairman, secretary, treasurer, turnout leaders)
and together comprise the IA committee.
Step 2 Formation of the IA. All TSA leaders, serving as elected representatives, ratify
theconstitution and by-laws of the IA and elect officers of the IAs as well as the
other members to the various committees that oversee the many functions of the
IA. These, inter alia, include:
-     By-laws: to review NIA standard by-laws and modify them as needed.
-     SEC Registration: to apply for secure legal status for the IA;
-     Water Permit: to apply for permission from the National Water Resources
Council to enable the IA to secure water rights and distribute water to
members
-     Rig1ht-of-y: to negotiate the right-of-way on behalf of the IA from the
members or others involved as the case may be.
-     Membership: to recruit and screen farmers for membership of the IA. The
membership fee is P5. In addition members pay an annual contribution of
PlO.
-     Finance and Audit,



- 138 -
Annex 8
Page 4 of 16
Others include Training, Service, and Grievance and Manpower Inventory
to establish manpower available to assist in construction.
Step 3 Registration of the IA with the Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC).
Step 4 Granting of NIA contracts, which is done after a series of meetings that lead to the
signing of a memorandum of understanding after which the IAs are eligible
toreceive contracts under the Management Turnover Program (MTP).
Policies and Procedures for the CIS
15.   Policies. Prior to commencement of a CIS, the IA is expected to comply with some basic
requirements. Membership of the IA is expected to be at least 80% of the total beneficiaries of
the scheme. The beneficiaries are expected to contribute at least 10% of the total cost in equity
through cash contributions, labor or right of way.
Procedures followed in Developing the CIS
16.   Detailed procedures have been developed, and documented, to guide the staff planning
and implementing CIS schemes and assist in the development of the lAs. The procedures are
formalized and provide the legal basis for transferring responsibility to the lAs.
17.   Once some farmers have expressed an interest in developing an irrigation scheme, the
IDO should be located in the village in about six to nine months in advance of scheme
development to organize the IA.
18.   Prior to designing the facility, a general layout is prepared the NIA and a walk-through is
conducted with farmers in each sector. Farmers' views are thus solicited prior to the presentation
of the layout design to the IA general assembly.
19.   Once the designs are approved, detailed estimates and the Plan of Work (POW) is
prepared as prerequisites for release of funds from NIA. This covers schedules for procurement
and delivery of materials, equipment, manpower and finance. The POW is also presented to the
IAs for suggestions prior to finalization. With the design and program of work finalized, some
two to four preconstruction conferences are held to agree on, and finalize: (a) the implementation
schedules; (b) IA's role and participation; and (c) the formation of construction committees. The
procedures and systems for construction are established and agreed upon, after which a
memorandum of understanding (MOU) is drawn up stipulating the rights and obligations of NIA
and the IA during the construction and the O&M phases with a view to assuring that the IA will
play a major role in providing needed manpower and help in executing the sub-project.
20.   At this point NIA also undertakes an IA viability study to assess its capabilities.
Assuming that the review is satisfactory, sub committees are mobilized to assist NIA in
procurement (Canvass, Bidding and Procurement), manpower mobilization, quality control, cost
control and mobilization of farmers' counterpart (equity).



- 139 -
Annex 8
Page 5 ofl6
21.   Prior to project turnover, a test-run of completed facilities is held jointly by NIA and the
IA. A further "walk through" and an inventory of facilities is prepared. Claims on technical
deficiencies are jointly investigated and, if found justified, a report is prepared. Repairs and
subsequent works arising from the joint investigation are not charged to the IA loan.
22.   An O&M  conference is held to review  procedures, identify O&M  needs and
responsibilities and appoint personnel or reactivate O&M committees as the case may be.
Turnover takes place within six months of sub-project completion. Financial turnover entails IA
acceptance of the total chargeable costs incurred and the amortization schedule. Physical
turnover entails the transfer and acceptance of facilities.
23.   At the start of the O&M, membership is updated, the organization of personnel is
reviewed (if necessary elections are held to replace the officer involved in the earlier stages) and
the by-laws revised. A system management plan is prepared comprising the proposed cropping
plan, a basic water distribution, a maintenance plan. At this point a second IA evaluation in also
undertaken, and if the IA is found viable, the IDO may be withdrawn although he or she may
continue contact with the IA on an intermittent basis.
IA Training
24.   The Training is geared to assisting the IA in managing the associations and the schemes.
The training modules, which have been developed with the experience gathered to date, include:
(a)   The Basic Leadership Development Course (BLDC). This three to four day course
is given immediately after the IA is established. It aims to strengthen the
leadership capabilities of its officers and emphasizes the process of group
dynamics, interaction and feedback, and skills needed in leadership.
(b)   Financial Management Training (FMT). The course, aimed at efficient
management of lAs finances, is provided before the project is formally handed
over. The three/four day course is given to the IA officers and the finance
committees members.
(c)   System Management Training (SMT) to improve the capability of the service
committee to operate and maintain the scheme.
25.   The above training modules have already been developed by the Head Office Staff, who
have, in turn trained selected Regional level staff to conduct the training programs. The IA
training is conducted at NIA's Regional Training Centers or some other training venue on a batch
basis. The emphasis is on formal training. Whenever considered necessary, some of the above
training modules are combined and given with other relevant courses with bookkeeping, audit
and other management aspects.



- 140 -
Annex 8
Page 6 of 16
The Management Turnover Contracts
26.  In the early stages of the development of the PAP, lAs were offered three types of
contracts that were meant to lead to progressively greater managerial responsibility in the
operation and maintenance of the irrigation schemes, namely:
Stage I     Maintenance Contracts, under which the lAs are responsible for
maintaining the canals at a contractual rate of P660/month/3.5 kms.
Stage II    The Joint System Management Contract, under which IAs take over the
O&M responsibilities within the IA area. The compensation paid to the IA
is based on the break-even point. ISF collections below the break-even
point are shared between NIA and the IA in the ratio of 60:40. If the ISF
collections exceed the break-even point, NIA receives 40% of the ISF and
the IA, 60%.
Stage III    Full Turnover Contracts, under which the IA is given full responsibility
for the O&M of the facility. Once the irrigation scheme is turned over, the
IA amortizes the chargeable costs of the scheme, much like the Communal
Irrigation Schemes.
27.   Following a review of the experience of the "Stage Contracts" in 1988, a new set of
management contracts were conceptualized to overcome some of the problems encountered as
the lAs tended to focus on ISF collection rather than maintenance of the facilities. particularly in
Stage II contracts. The new contracts are referred to as Types I, II and Type III. lAs can opt
either for the Type I or Type II or Type I and II combined.
Type I      Maintenance Contract, which is similar to the Stage 1 contract although
the lAs are given a higher compensation of P1,100/month/3.5kms to
maintain the canals. The higher rate offered reflects the increased
maintenance costs.
Type II     ISF contract under which the lAs assist (campaign for payment or collect)
in the collection of payments from the farmers. The incentive structure is
as follows:
Collection           Incentives
below 50%                0%
50 - 60%               2%
60 - 70%                5%
70 - 90%               10%
90 -100%               15%
back accounts          25%



- 141 -
Annex 8
Page 7 of 16
Type III    Full Turnover Contract, which is essentially similar to the Stage III
contract.
Status of MTP Coverage
28.   Since the inception of the program, the coverage in terms of numbers, has been
impressive. As of December 1994, NIA had organized a total 4,474 IAs reaching a coverage of
89% of the targeted 4,978 IAs (see Table 2) that are to be formed under the MTP for both NIS
and the CIS. Some 89% of the targets have been met in CIS schemes and the corresponding
figure in case of NIS is 92%. Some 1,827 groups have been organized (94.4% coverage) in NIS
on an area of over 552,000 ha with over 444,000 farmers. Of these, 1,778 lAs have been
registered and 1,542 had been given contracts but some have not renewed them. As a result, as of
May 1995, some 1,520 lAs had contracts.
29.   The IAs seem to have received more of the Type I, Type II, Stage II and combined Type I
and Type II contracts rather than either Stage I or Type III/Stage III contracts. Stage I is less
popular as lAs receive lower payments than the Type I contracts. As of December 1993, of the
1,520 IAs that had received contracts, 478 (32%) had received for Type I contracts (Table 3).
The corresponding figures for Stage I, Stage II, Type II and Types I and II combined), were 15
(1%), 100 (7%), 411 (27%) and 474 (31%) respectively. It is interesting to observe, however,
that very few lAs --only 41 lAs (2.7%) -- were working with Type III and Stage III contracts,
under which the lAs have full responsibility for ISF collections and for the O&M of their
schemes. In this sense, the PAP as is it being implemented at present, has achieved only limited
success.
30.   The broad breakdown analyzed by the type of responsibilities that IAs have undertaken to
handle are as follows:
Responsibility         Type of Contracts   Numbers    % of Total
Maintenance       Type I and Stage I       493  32.4
Oper/ISF Coll.             Type II         986  64.9
Full responsibility       Stage III         41   2.7
Total                       1,520          100.0
Reviews of the IDP
31.   There have been selected studies to evaluate either the performance of the PAP in general
or selected modifications to the program. These include:



- 142 -
Annex 8
Page 8 of 16
1)    The Management Turnover of a Pump Irrigation System in The Philippines: The
Farmers Way l/. The study based on the experience of a single Pump Irrigation
Scheme in the Angat Masim Rivers Irrigation System concluded that:
(a)   employing selected and well trained farmers in organizing co-farmers can
lead to active lAs at the field and distributory levels at lower costs and
with better collection efficiency.
(b)   Compared with the previous approach with IDOs, use of FIOs in
organizing activities can be shortened, made less expensive and be
effective.
(c)   rehabilitation and institutional development must go hand-in-hand.
2)    Farmers participation in National Irrigation Systems in the Philippines: Lessons
Learned /. The study concluded that:
a)    IA development requires change, which is at times resisted by those
implementing the program. Firm policies and support are required at the
highest level to promote participatory approaches.
(b)   In order to make farmers' participation effective, farmers have to be
organized by methods that build capacity for decision making during the
organizing process. From this point of view, the procedures followed in
CIS are more effective than those followed in the NIS.
3)    An Evaluation of the Impact of the Farmers's Participation in the NIS
Performance 3/.
This study attempted to assess of the impact of the PAP on 77 NIS schemes. The
IAs selected had been organized between 1982 and 1991 and the objective was to
compare the performance of the MTP and the non-MTP systems in terms of
collection efficiency (CE), repair and maintenance costs (RM), personnel costs
(PC), dry season benefitted area (DSBA) and dry season rice yields (DSRY). It
appears that on all accounts, the MTP schemes fared better than non-MTP
schemes. The CE was 60% in MTP areas and 43% in non-MTP areas. The RM
costs were, on average, some 30% lower in the MTP areas than the non-MTP
areas -- the figures for 1991 being P380/ha and P540/ha respectively. The PC
costs in MTP areas were also lower (P340/ha vs. P450/ha) which, the study
IIMI Country Paper - The Philippines - No. 2
2/NIACONSULT, INC. December 1993.
3/NIACONSULT, INC.- 1993.



- 143 -
Annex 8
Page 9 of 16
attributed to the displacement of the ditchtenders. The DSBA in MTP schemes
increased by 40% as compared to 20% for non MTP areas.
II. ISSUES
32.   There is little doubt that the PAP program has been successful in achieving a number of
the objectives established at the inception of the program (better water distribution, lower costs
and better CE). However, a number of problems have arisen and need to be addressed to secure
the accomplishments achieved so far and, where possible, improve on the base already
developed. These are:
(a)   Voluntary Membership. Not all farmers served by the TSBA are members,
making it difficult for the leaders to enforce discipline on all farmers in a TSBA.
NIA has taken the position that in view of the past experience in Philippines with
farmers groups, participation in a TSBA should be voluntary. Valid as this
argument may be, the failure to enforce discipline among members is proving
expensive for NIA and some other solution needs to be explored.
(b)   Weak IAs. Not all IAs that have received contracts are strong and coherent. Data
on the number of lAs that are effective are not available, but it is becoming
increasingly apparent that some 50% of the lAs developed so far are facing
difficulties in fulfilling the terms of their contracts leaving NIA in a difficult
position. On one hand, NIA must pay the lAs to fulfill its obligations under the
contract and, on the other, it continues to incur additional expenditures to collect
funds and maintain the facilities.
(c)   Failure to Enforce the Terms of the Contracts. In a joint management set-up such
as the NIS it is important that both parties meet their contractual obligations,
failing which either party should have some recourse to compensation. NIA has to
explore ways to enforce the terms of the contract through sanctions/legal means or
better training.
(d)   Frequent Changes in Institutional Arrangements in liaising with the lAs. When
PAP was initiated, NIA employed degree holders -- (IDOs) -- to train the lAs. In
an effort to reduce costs, NIA displaced the IDOs with Watermasters who were
supported by FIOs in organizing the lAs. These changes had two negative aspects.
Firstly, the lAs lost contact with the people they trusted which had a bearing on
their attitude towards NIA. Secondly, although the FIOs were expected to
continue in advisory capacity as ex-officio members even after their stipend was
terminated, some have ceased to play this role. Farmers are generally suspicious
by nature and continued contact through trusted officials is important to ensure
both the formation of coherent lAs and to take timely action if the IA is being
misled.
(e)   Inadequate Training. Leaders are trained once and the members receive limited
training to monitor the leadership and take timely action if the leadership falters.



- 144 -
Annex 8
Page lO of 16
It is important to stress that the institutional development of IAs is a long-term
process and continued support is necessary to secure their sound development.
(f)    Conflict of Interest in NIA with Staff implementing the Program. The long-term
development of the IAs was jeopardized by NIA staff members, who feared that
they might lose their jobs if the IAs successfully implemented their contractual
obligations. The categories of staff directly effected were the IDOs and the
Ditchtenders. The ICO/IDOs were employed on a contractual basis, with their
employment terminating once the lAs were trained. Some of them, realizing that
their jobs would be terminated, deliberately did not train the lAs to ensure their
continued dependence. Further, the Ditchtenders had most to lose with the transfer
of maintenance responsibilities to the lAs and they also attempted to delay the
process.
(g)   Lack of Financial Resources has resulted in some disillusionment with the system.
Some IAs have now begun to return their maintenance contracts as NIA is unable
to pay either the IAs share of the ISF or the payments of the maintenance
contracts on time.
(h)   Shortage of Staff in NIS. The total number of IDOs working full time on the NIS
is limited. The WRFTs are supposed to facilitate the development of the lAs, but
they seem to be pre-occupied with water distribution and ISF collections. The
WRFTs perceive their role in IA development as being one aimed at legal
compliance with SEC requirements and the by-laws rather than taking a
comprehensive view of the strengths and weaknesses and addressing them either
with the support of the IDOs or through discussions with the leaders and the
members as the case may be. It was generally presumed that the FIOs once
trained would continue to act in advisory capacity, even after their stipend was
terminated. Experience has proved otherwise and some other form of
compensation would need to be explored to ensure their continued participation in
IA development.
(i)   Lack of Incentive for Leadership to be involved in ISF Collections. The IA by-
laws specifically prohibit the leadership from receiving any incentives or
compensation for spending their time on collection of ISF. It is difficult for any
one to continue contributing their time day-after-day without some remuneration,
which perhaps explains the reasons behind the IAs failure to effectively handle
Type II or Stage II contracts without support from NIA.
(j)   Lack of Comprehensive Plan for Ensuring that lAs Progressively Handle Greater
Responsibifliy. The PAP, as originally conceived had envisioned that the lAs
would progressively move from Stage I to Stage III contracts. For some
inexplicable reason, the program seems to have lost its momentum to
progressively transfer greater responsibility to the IAs.



- 145 -
Annex 8
Page 11 of 16
(k)   Inadequate Monitoring. The IDP monitoring is largely geared to reporting on
events planned for any fiscal year, rather than on the progress in tackling specific
problems faced by the IAs. This approach need to be reviewed, specifically with
respect to reporting on the number of lAs that are weak and unable to fulfil the
terms of their contracts.
(1)   Failure to Adhere to Established Polices and Procedures in Development of CIS.
Data on all CIS financed by NIA are not available, but from the limited data
available from projects financed under CIDP 2, it appears that the policies and
procedures established under CIS are not begin adhered to. Of the 83 schemes
initiated under CIDP II as of December 31, 1994, 22 had membership below the
80% required to develop a CIS. While the combined equity paid 8.34% of the
total cost (in contrast to the 10% required) in all the 83 schemes, this figure is
misleading as the amount paid in 14 schemes was below 5% and in one, as little
as 1.7%. It seems that the IDOs are no longer working with the lAs in CIS about
six to nine months in advance of initiating the construction as required by the
established procedures because the funds are released late, and this perhaps
accounts for the lower than required membership and equity contribution. The
expediency of developing schemes should not over ride the need to adhere to
established procedures as it could lead to lower arnortization payments.
In developing CIS schemes, NIA is ultimately granting a loan to the IAs and it is
important that the beneficiaries realize that NIA expects them to meet their
obligations at all stages of the development of the schemes. To this end, the
insistence on following established polices and procedures would demonstrate
NIA's resolve to recover its costs.
(m)   Lack of Established Policy on Past Dues Prior to Rehabilitation of CIS. It appears
that there is no established policy with respect to clearance of past dues when CIS
are rehabilitated. Such a policy needs to be formulated.
(n)   Outlining of Collection Procedures. The training provided to lAs does not include
training on procedures to be followed in collecting ISF overdues from its
members. These need to introduced in the training given to the members prior to
handing over the schemes.
III. SUMMARY
33.   There is little doubt that the basic framework to transfer responsibility to the IAs has
already been initiated. In fact, when measured by volume, the number of IAs formed, registered
and granted contracts is impressive. Although there is no clear picture regarding the number of
lAs that are capable of fulfilling the terms of their contracts without close supervision from NIA,
it seems increasingly apparent that a number of them are not performing up to expectations.
Preliminary results of the functionality survey conducted by the IDD in late 1995, albeit only
partial as some of the Regions had not submitted their reports, indicated that some 10% of
the lAs were very functional, another 50% moderately functional and 40% were non functional.



- 146 -
Annex 8
Page 12 of 16
On the other hand, it appears that there are a number of good lAs that can easily handle greater
responsibility, but are not doing so for some reason or another.
34.   In brief, the IA development program has, in a sense, lost momentum. More of the same
is being offered in terms of training, interaction with lAs and granting of contracts without a
comprehensive action plan to progressively encourage the lAs to undertake greater
responsibility. One example of this would be NIA billing the lAs as one entity, leaving it for
them to bill and collect from their members. This approach is not being actively pursued even
though it would be a logical step in preparation for NIA's long-term objective of wholesaling
water at the head of the lateral.
35.   As already recognized and accepted within NIA, the objective should be to ultimately
wholesale water to the lAs as and when they become fully trained to accept the responsibility of
managing the whole scheme. This important objective, which could result in savings for NIA and
the IAs, would be met only if the lAs are well established coherent entities, capable of enforcing
discipline on members and executing the terms of their contracts with NIA.
36.   If the aforementioned objective is to be met, IDP would need to be reformulated.
Considerable modifications in the incentive framework, training programs and, equally
importantly, the present approach to interfacing with the lAs would have to be made to achieve
this target. Specifically, the following aspects would need to be considered and introduced.
(a)   Incentives would need to be built-in to ensure that the IA leadership is suitably
compensated for its efforts in collecting the ISF.
(b)   There is a need to encourage greater participation by the members in decision
making while ensuring, at the same time, that the leaders have the capability to
enforce discipline on the members. In order to achieve this form of interaction, the
IDOs have to maintain close contact with the leaders and the members.
(c)   The IA instruction would need to focus on training the lAs for direct billing and
eventually for delivery of water services by NIA at the lateral head, and, in
selected NIS, for compete turnover. Moreover, the whole conceptual approach to
training would need to shift to encourage, in addition to formal training, informal
training through visits. Where possible, the leadership of the more successful lAs
would need to be encouraged to interact with the leadership of the weaker lAs.
(d)   In some cases, the endorsement of the Barangay leadership may become
necessary to prop up the leadership of the IA if it performs poorly in collecting
ISF.
(e)   In extreme cases, it may even become necessary to merge some IAs.
(f)    Because the problems faced by the IAs may differ, the IDOs would have to be
trained to recognize the problems and mobilize the leadership or the membership,
as the case may be, to respond to a wide range of issues.



- 147 -
Annex 8
Page 13 of 16
(g)   The reporting system would need to be revised to ensure that the data presented to
the management reflects the reality on the ground.
(h)   The policies and procedures should be modified taking into account the points
made in para. 31 (1),(m),and (n)).
IV. THE PROPOSED IA DEVELOPMENT COMPONENT
Approach
37.   Clearly the resuscitation of the IA development program will have to be initiated at the
grassroot level with the primary objective of ensuring group action and cohesion as the minimum
requirements for ensuring discipline among members. The objective would be to secure support
of the members in enforcing sanctions against recalcitrant members, while strengthening, at the
same time, the understanding of the members to monitor and control the leaders. If this basic, but
difficult, objective can be achieved at the TSA level, the foundation will have been laid to
develop strong lAs and their subsequent federation into larger tertiary level organizations.
38.  In order to secure this fundamental principle certain organizational changes will have to
be introduced. The IDOs will have to interface with the IA leadership systematically and, in
cases where the TSAs are weak, the TSA membership and leadership will have to retrained to
establish the basic principles outlined in the above paragraph.
ReQuirements
39.   The strengthening of the TSAs/IAs would require that the IDOs will have to be regularly
present at the meetings of the members, directing the affairs of the IAs in a way that all decisions
have popular support and that decisions are seen to be made by the members. This crucial point,
although basic to achieving group discipline, is not easily attained. The IDOs would need to have
a personality profile that readily wins over the confidence of the members. Moreover, they would
have to interface with the members in a way that he/she directs the group to a common cause and
purpose without appearing to do so.
40.   The approach would need time to develop. The IDOs have to be initially present at all the
IA meetings and it can take at least 12 to 18 months before a cohesive IA can emerge. The initial
IA training modules already developed by NIA would have to be supplemented by frequent visits
by the IDOs: it is anticipated that an IDO working on a fortnightly visit schedule could handle 8
lAs at a time (on the assumption that they would visit the field Monday through Thursday,
leaving Fridays for office work). It is also expected that when an IA is considered to be
functional, the intensity of the visits can be reduced and the IDO can be redeployed to work with
another IA.
41.   The training of the staff to introduce this approach would have to be conducted by the
proposed Group Organizer TA to be recruited under IOSP II. The TA would have to undertake
an active training program to assist: (a) the Regional staff to supervise the IDOs and train them
using the approach outlined above; (b) the IDOs on-the-job when they are attending IA meetings.



- 148 -
Annex 8
Page 14 of 16
42.   Unlike the past when a number of temporary staff were assigned to undertake
institutional development with poor results, the advancement of the IA development program
will have to done by a core of well trained staff that have security of tenure and are exclusively
assigned to undertake institutional development. Further, it is important that these staff be no
longer involved in ISF collections.
43.  It is, therefore, recommended that NIA develop a cadre of 174 well trained IDOs (based
on a workload of 12.5 IAs/IDO) to support IA development in the NIS. As there are only 40 IDO
positions approved, most of the staff assigned to undertake institutional work would have to be
given related positions that have been approved but remain unfilled. The Senior WRFT positions
(with assigned responsibility for undertaking, among other things, institutional development
work) seems to be ideal from this point of view. At present some 1,063 positions are authorized
for WRFTs, of which 591 are filled in. On average, therefore, NIA carries one WRFT for every
1,100 ha. Of the 472 positions vacant, some 88 positions are for Senior WRFTs, and the
remainder for WRFTs. Clearly NIA has a sufficient number of WRFT positions authorized to
select/recruit adequately qualified positions to undertake the institutional development work (see
Tables 1 and 2, and also 6 of the Project File Working Paper No. 2).
44.   Not all these positions would be required, however. At present, there are 17 staff
undertaking Institutional Development work in the head office. They are supported by 75 staff
(on COB) in the Regions and 42 IDOs recruited under IOSP II (see Table 1). Of the 75 staff
undertaking institutional development work in the Regions, 18 senior staff would be required at
the regional level to act as Senior IDOs to coordinate institutional development work and
supervise other IDOs in their respective regions, while the other 57 can be redeployed to
undertake institutional development work in the field. As there are already 57 staff available on
the COB in the Regions, only 117 additional positions would be required. These are far below
the number of WRFT positions available (472) and the proposed program can be implemented
without increasing the number of positions.
45.   The availability of these staff suggests that some 117 additional staff would need to be
identified. These can be recruited form the 42 temporary IDOs (who can be brought on the
plantillia in the Senior WRFT Positions) and the other 75 can be promoted from the WRFTs,
some of whom are very good at institutional work and can be easily retrained to follow the
approach outlined in paras 39 and 40. Where necessary, a salary supplement can be granted to
these staff for undertaking duties beyond their immediate terms of employment -- the
Government's procedures pernit payment of such supplements.
46.   However, it is also important that the NIA's payroll not be increased. It is, therefore,
recommended that the 117 WRFT positions that would be filled under the program be offset by
corresponding reductions in the number of WRFTs on the plantillia from 591 to 434 (see Tables
5 and 6 of the Project File Working Paper No. 2). This would increase the workload on the
remaining from the present 1,100 ha to 1,500 ha -- a workload that is considered manageable as
the remaining WRFTs would not be undertaking institutional development work anymore.
47.   If the proposed plan of work is accepted, the job descriptions of the Senior WRFTs
assigned to undertake institutional development and those Senior WRFTs or other WRFTs



- 149 -
Annex 8
Page 15 of 16
assigned to work exclusively as Watermasters would have to be revised. These are given in
Attachments 1, 2 and 3 respectively.
48.  In case of really poorly functioning lAs, it may become necessary to deploy the FIOs --
unfortunately on a temporary basis -- to work with the IDOs and this would have to be built in
the program. Some 2,608 FIOs may be required in the long term.
49.   Moreover, the program would have to be target driven to ensure that lAs are given
progressively greater responsibility. Each IDO would be responsible for progressively moving
the lAs from Type I/Stage I to Type II/Stage II and Type III/Stage III contracts within a specified
time period, failing which they would have to provide suitable explanations. The system would
have adequate checks and balance to ensure that the IDOs do not play the "numbers game" and
that the lAs are given greater responsibility only when they are ready for it. In order to achieve
this objective, the Regional level Senior IDOs would closely supervise all the IDOs in the field
visiting them while they are attending IA meetings and independently assessing the LAs when the
IDOs recommend them for the next contract Type.
50.   The development of this approach would require three essential conditions: namely: (a)
the job descriptions of the Senior IDOs be revised; (b) an effective monitoring system be
developed; and (c) the staff concerned be assured security of tenure, otherwise, as in the past,
they would be reluctant to transfer responsibilities to the lAs.
51.   The overall plans and targets for training the proposed 2,282 lAs to Type III/Stage III
contracts in the NIS, including those already formed and those that have yet to be developed, are
given in Tables 8 through 12 in the Project File Working Paper No. 2.
The Systems Management Committees and Farmers' Participation
52.   The development of an active participatory program, involving the beneficiaries from
planning through implementation is also essential. Although the approach would be somewhat
slower in the decision making, the longer-term benefits of improved prospects for sustaining the
development are expected to offset the costs in delayed implementation.
53.   To encourage participation, NIA proposes to formalize the existing NIA- IA dialogue
through the formation of the Systems Management Committees (SMCs), which would be formed
to: (a) reflect the farmers' views and experiences in the management of the schemes; (b) reach an
agreement on the key aspects to improve the systems; and (c) to promote an active NIA - IA
partnership. Specifically, the SMCs undertake to (a) plan the overall activities; (b) identify and
implement proposed improvements along the same lines as the CIS; (c) inspect and approve, and
(in the case of the IAs) accept completed improvement works; and (d) monitor and evaluate the
individual IAs and the overall system performance.
54.   Each SMC would comprise the Irrigation Superintendent or the District Chief (IS/DC),
Presidents of the IAs and the Heads of the Finance, billing and Collection Sections in the IS.
Outside agencies may be invites as and when necessary. The SMC would be chaired by the
IS/DC and the Vice Chairman would be one of the presidents of the lAs.



- 150 -
Annex 8
Page 16 of 16
55.    The approach is basically sound. With some minor modifications in the contracts
proposed (for example, clarification of the actions open to NIA, if the IA fails to perforn), the
SMC approach should be tried in all the 18 schemes under IOSP II. If considered replicable, the
SMC concept would be expanded to systems under WRDP and other NIS.
The Proposed Project Component
56.   The objective of the component would be to develop the 2,207 lAs in NIA (that is,
excluding those covered by ISOP II) to undertake either Type III or Joint management contracts
effectively by the time WRDP is completed.
57.   Project costs would include funds for class room training of the lAs (financial
management, basic leadership, systems operation and monitoring) which would be supplemented
by on-the-job training by the IDOs. Project costs would also include funds for initial re-training
of the IDOs using the approach outlined above, six monthly seminars for IDOs which would be
based on case studies and an active information exchange program. In addition, funds have been
provided to recruit about 1,000 FIOs for a period of one year each to assist the IDOs at the TSA
level in case of very weak lAs. About 830 FIOs are expected to be recruited for the WRDP and
the rest for other NIS. The costs and phasing of the component for WRDP is given in Table 13 of
the Project File Working Paper No. 2. The unit cost of the training is given in Attachment 4.
58.   Project funds would also be provided for developing an effective monitoring and
evaluation system (3 person-months of consultancies) for tracking the development of the ]As. In
addition, it is anticipated that as the lAs become strong they would be federated into larger
bodies that would undertake system operation and maintenance of larger schemes. Some 12
person-months of consultancies would be required for developing these Federations, capitalizing
them and in initiating their operations.
59.   The following measures would be essential to successfully implement the IA
development program:
(a)   the identification and placement of the 57 IDOs to be transferred from the ROs to
the field; and
(b)   the identification and training of the WRFTs that would be working as IDOs. It is
important that all staff handling institutional development work have a personality
profile given in para 39. All staff affected by the program, including the WRFTs
that operate only as Watermasters, should be given their revised job descriptions
before they undertake their new duties.



- 151 -
ANNEX8
Attachment 1
The Proposed Responsibilities of the Water Resources Facilities
Technicians (WRFT)
In close coordination with the lAs, the WRFT would supervise and operate, the
irrigation facilities under his area of control, productive and efficient. Specifically, the
WRFT would assist the IS to:
(a)   orient the farmers, through the lAs, in NIA's policies and guidelines on
irrigation operations;
(b)   assist the IS, with participation of the IAs in planning O&M activities;
(c)   implement the O&M plan in cooperation with the lAs and the members;
(d)   maintain operations records for his area, and submit consolidate reports,
based on IA operations records, on irrigated and planted areas, water
deliveries and distribution, crop failures, harvested/benefitted areas,
payments of ISF fees and damages to irrigation facilities and structures;
(e)   In selected lAs with only maintenance contracts, supervise through the
lAs, the distribution of ISF bills to the farmers; and
(g)   perform other related functions as may be required by the IS.



- 152 -
ANNEX8
Attachment 2
The Proposed Responsibilities of the Institutional
Development Officers (IDOs)
In close coordination with the Senior Institutional Development Officers develop
the lAs to manage their own internal affairs and progressively handle greater
responsibility, gradually moving form undertaking maintenance contracts to maintenance
and ISF collection and finally, managing the entire area under their control. In the context
the IDO would:
(a)    work with TSA members to identify the right leaders that would represent
them in the lAs;
(b)    guide, without appearing to do so, to question the leadership about the
affairs the lAs;
(c)    enhance group awareness in enforcing discipline among non-performing
or non-paying members;
(d)    assist the leadership in maintaining records and present them regularly to
the members;
(e)    ensure compliance with all SEC requirements;
(f)    assist the WRFTs to orient farmers in NIAs policies and guidelines and
operations;
(g)    conduct IA functionality survey, evaluate the performance of the lAs and
its leadership and recommend them for training programs, when
necessary; and
(h)    undertake any other activity that the IS or the SDO may require.



- 153 -
ANNEXS
Attachment 3
The Proposed Responsibilities of the Senior Institutional
Development Officers (SDOs)
The SDO would provide regional support to Irrigation systems offices in
undertaking IA development. Specifically, the SDO would:
(a)   supervise all IA development activity in the regions;
(b)   monitor the performance of all IA and provide periodic reports to the
Institutional Development Department in the head office.
(c)   conduct/arrange for all training of the IA members and their officers as
necessary;
(d)   conduct/arrange training for IDOs when necessary;
(e)   from time to time visit all IDOs in the field and supervise them when they
are conducting meetings in the field;
(f)   develop a program to systematically give greater responsibility to the lAs
and monitor its progress;
(g)   personally assess those lAs that are recommended for a progressively
greater responsibility before such contracts are granted; and
(h)   undertake any other responsibility that the ROs or the IDD may, from time
to time, require.

IA/IDO TRAINING
All the training programs for the SDO shall be initiated by the IDOs with the
assistance of the SDOs. The IDO will provide the post-training guidance to the 1A
officers and members that have undergone training.
I1.   In areas without IA contract, the IA shall be provided the following training
programs:
(a) Basic Leadership and Organizational Discipline (BLOD) - for IA Officers (3
days);
(b) Financial Management (FM) - for IA President, Vice-Presidents, and TSA
Leaders (3 days);
(c)   Basic Systems Operations and Monitoring (BSOM) - for IA Presidents,
Vice-Presidents, and TSA Leaders (r days);
(d)   Basic Agricultural Support Services Supervision  and Monitoring
(BASSSM) - for IA Presidents, Vice-Presidents and TSA Leaders - (2
days); and
(e)   Basic  Systems  Operation,  Agricultural  Support  Services  and
Organizational Operation and Discipline (BSOASSOD) - for all IA
members (2 days).
2.   In areas with IA with or without contract, the las shall be provided the appropriate
development training programs. However, these will be based on:
(a)   the results of the IA functionality survey;
(b)   the present NIA-IA management contracts they have; and
(c)   the next NIA-IA management contract they would to have.
3.    The development training programs will include the following:
(a)   Maintenance Supervision and Monitoring (MSM) - for IA officers and
TSA Leaders (2 days);
(b)   Operation and tSA Supervision and Monitoring (OISM) - for IA Officers
and TSA Leaders (e days);



- 155 -
ANNEX8
Attachment 4
Page 2 of 3
(c)   Systems Operation and Monitoring (SOM) - for IA Officers and TSA
Leaders (5 days);
(d)   Organizational Policies and Discipline (OPD) - for IA Officers and TSA
Leaders (3 days); and
(e)   Systems Operation, Agricultural Support Services and Organizational
Discipline Orientation (SOASSOD) - for IA Members (3 days).
4.    In view of the changes in the way systems operation is undertaken with the lAs
gradually taking over responsibilities, the IDO shall likewise be trained in:
(a)   Systems Operation and Monitoring (SOM) - 4 days;
(b)   IA Management and Supervisions (IMS) - 3 days; and
(c)   Basic Post-Training Support and Evaluation (BPTSE) - 3 days.
5.    Likewise, the regional staff who will provide the guidance and support to the field
staff shall be provided training by the Central Office Staff and these shall include
planning and supervision of:
(a)   Systems Operation and Monitoring (SOM) - 5 days;
(b)   IA Management and Supervision (IMS) - 4 days; and
(c)   Basic Post-Training Support and Evaluation (BPTSE) - 4 days.
6.    The per unit cost of the various training programs is given in the following Table.

ANNEX 9
FPEPINS
WATER RESOURCESR DEVEL,OPMENT PROJECT
Draft TOR for NIA's Environment Unit
1.      It is considered important for NIA to do something to better institutionalize environmental and
watershed management within the organization, both to improve the effectiveness of existing watershed
management activities and its ability to secure environmental approvals for new developments in the
future. The potential scope of responsibilities of an environmental/watershed management Unit or
Division is suggested in the table below. The most important means of implementing these suggestions
is to institutionalize environmental and watershed management capacity within NIA by creating a
permanent Unit or Division within either PDD or SMD for implementing such work and to increase the
number of permanent positions assigned to such work. No technical assistance is required to take these
actions, although there is little doubt that any decisions on strengthening environmental management
capacity in NIA would be better informed if a suitable needs assessment was carried out. Provision has
been made in the project for 3 months of consultancy support to: (a) carry out an in-depth analysis of
NIA's environmental assessment and management (including watershed management) needs as a basis
for preparing recommendations to NIA's senior management on the establishment and staffing of a
permanent Unit or Division within NIA; and (b) provide some on-the-job training in environmental
impact assessment to the staff of the Unit or Division. The consultancy could be combined with that
proposed for the implementation of the sediment monitoring activities funded under the project.
Area of Activity                                      Particulars
Environmental Managament    * formulation, review, revision and update of NIA corporate environmental policies;
* preparation of Initial Environmental Examinations, Project Descriptions,
Environmental Characterization, and Environmental Impact Statements for new NIA
developments;
* managing all environmental approval procedures on behalf of NIA (liaison with
DENR, public consultations, etc.);
* in-house training and technical assistance on environmental design, management
and approvals procedures;
* technical assistance on design of environmental monitoring and management plans
for new and existing developments in accordance with ECC conditionalities;
* preparation of environmental management guidelines for use by field units;
Environmental Monitoring     * auditing/supervision of implementation of environmental management and
monitoring plans;
* reporting of ECC conditionality compliance to DENR/LGU/NGOs;
* carrying out and/or supervising environmental monitoring of new and existing
developments;
* data collation and review;
Watershed Management         * characterization of watersheds of irrigation projects for input to project feasibility
studies and design of watershed management plans;
* erosion assessments in watersheds of existing irrigation schemes to provide a basis
for ameliorative work;
* sedimentation assessments (sources and quantities);
* assessment of effectiveness of watershed management plans by field inspections
and monitoring;
* provision of technical assistance to NIA field and project staff to improve
implementation effectiveness;
* coordination with DENR and others regarding cooperative management programs;
e provision of advice to relevant councils and agencies regarding watershed land
uses and other activities which are incompatible with NIA objectives; and
* training and technology transfer to NIA field staff on all matters relating to
watershed management.

- 186 -
ANNEX 13
Page 1 of 2
PHILIPPINES
WATER RESOURCES DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Monitoring and Evaluation
1.    The project has multiple objectives and some of its development impacts will not be easily
measurable. This applies in particular to improved water resources planning and management,
improved watershed management, and institutional strengthening of NIA and the lAs. It is,
therefore, necessary to have some proxy indicators, which would provide a reasonable basis to
conclude that the project achieved its intended development impact. Appendix I provides a matrix
of project objectives; key performance indicators; estimated values (where applicable) for baseline
(1995), mid-term (end of 1999), implementation completion report (year 2002), and full impact (year
2004); and risks. The measurability of development impact of different components can be easily
assessed from the matrix. Project inputs have not been shown in Appendix I since they are covered
in the cost tables in Annex 10. The Progress Reports to be prepared by the implementing agencies
will include information on project inputs and processes, as well as project outputs. Based on this
information and the Bank review during supervision, assessment of the project development impact
will be made by the Borrower and the Bank.
2.    Given multiple components and the difficulties of impact measurement for some
components, an overall assessment of the project development impact during implementation, at
completion and after completion will necessarily be imprecise. It is proposed to facilitate the overall
assessment by assigning weights to the different components as follows:
Development
Impact
Component                                                     Weight
Improved Water Resource Management                              20
Improved Watershed Management                                   15
Irrigation Systems Improvement and Repair                       35
Institutional Strengthening of NIA and the IAs                  20
Environmental Improvement                                       10
Total                                                 lo0
3.    The above weights, which are subjective, reflect the importance assigned at appraisal to the
objective of the component within the overall project framework, and have no relationship to the
project costs incurred for the component.  A total score of 80 and above during project
implementation and at completion should be regarded as a highly satisfactory project development
impact, 51 to 79 as satisfactory, 35 to 50 as unsatisfactory, and less than 35 as highly unsatisfactory.
4.    To further facilitate the overall assessment of project development impact, key performance
indicators were chosen (Appendix 1) and assigned weights (within the overall weights shown in
para. 2 above). The weights of key performance indicators (some of which are proxy indicators) are
detailed below:



- 187 -
ANNEX 13
Page 2 of 2
(a)    For the improved water resources management component, the development impact
is extremely difficult to assess. One of the project outputs, formulation of a Nat:onal
Water Resources Plan, would be the main (proxy) indicator, since this project output
has the best potential at this stage to improve water resources management in the
country. The indicator would have a score of 10. Other indicators would be: (i) the
strengthening of NWRB (score of 5); (ii) improvement of national water data
collection (score of 3); and (iii) establishment of a water information network (score
of 2). Any shortfalls in the above indicators would result in a component score lower
than 20.
(b)    For the improved watershed management component, the same problem  of
assessment arises and, again, one of the project outputs, formulation of a national
strategy and long-term investment and institutional strengthening program for
watershed management, was chosen as the key (proxy) indicator, with a score of 10.
Since the impact of investments in improving the management of selected high
priority watersheds, to be undertaken after the completion of the study above, is
difficult to envisage at this stage, a score of 5 is assigned to this sub-component.
(c)    For the irrigation systems improvement sub-component, a score of 30 would be
assigned if the increase in irrigated area in the improved systems equals or exceeds
appraisal expectations, or there is sufficient evidence to believe that the irrigated area
will equal or exceed appraisal expectations within a year or two after project
completion.  The increase in irrigated area is a proxy indicator for increased
production, income and employment of small farmers and landless agricultural
workers, which would have a significant impact on rural poverty alleviation. The
repair of major structures and construction of silt excluders (also proxy indicators)
would have a score of 5. Any shortfalls in the above indicators would result in a
component score of less than 35.
(d)    For the institutional development component, the full score of 20 would be assigned
if, by the end of the project: (i) 19 small NIS (improved under the project ancd the
ongoing IOSP II) are completely turned over to the lAs and the responsibility for
O&M of laterals and sub-laterals of other systems improved under the proje-t is
turned over to the lAs; and (ii) NIA's total income from ISF and equipment rental
from the 14 selected systems is sufficient to cover O&M expenses of these NIS. The
above two key indicators will have equal weight. Any shortfalls in achievement
would lower the component score from 20.
(e)    For the environmental improvement component, a score of 5 would be assigned if the
reduction in the schistosomiasis prevalence rate envisaged at appraisal for two NIS is
achieved and the prevalence rate for one NIS is maintained. This would be the key
indicator. Other relatively minor indicators (satisfactory erosion control works in 31
NIS and sediment monitoring in 3 NIS, and establishment of an Environment Unit in
NIA), which are not shown in Appendix 1, would have a score of 5.
5.    Appendix I also shows the risks of non-achievement of project outputs and impact. These
risks are discussed in greater detail in para. 6.14 of the SAR and have been taken into account in the
economic and sensitivity analysis (para. 6.11 of the SAR).

PHILIPPINES
Water Resources DeveloDment Proiect
Water Availability for NIS and Possible Increase in Irrieation
1.    The water availability at each of the 14 NIS selected for improvement has been
examined for its sufficiency for the target crop areas in the service area under the
anticipated cropping pattern. In general, the NIS can be divided into two categories: 12
NIS depending on the natural flow in the rivers from which they draw water, and the other
2 for which water is allocated from the storages (Magat and Angat) constructed on the
source streams. Besides the main source of water, most of the NIS also divert water from
smaller streams traversing the service area or located in the vicinity. The releases from
Magat are primarily for irrigation, power generation being dependent on the pattern of
irrigation requirements. However, the available water at Angat has to be shared between
irrigation and Manila water supply, power generation depending on the pattern of the two
requirements. Though allocations are clearly defined at the storages, problems arise due
to the priority for Manila water supply demands, and occasional energy crisis.
2.       An assessment of the supplies available from the main source of each NIS was
carried out earlier as one of the nine tasks identified for project preparation. Under Task
1, the 10 day stream  flows at 80% availability were estimated. In a few cases, the data
generated was based on very limited information and, therefore, had low reliability. The
analysis carried out by the project preparation team is based on the Task 1 results. The
flow available in most of the smaller streams traversing the service area, which is used for
irrigation, is the best judgement of the NIS staff, except where data could be generated.
3.         The cropping pattern at each NIS is invariably paddy in each season. The
irrigation requirements for paddy have been estimated on the basis of pan evaporation
and rainfall recorded at the nearest reliable meteorological station. For all NIS included in
the project, the paddy evapotranspiration is taken as 110% of pan evaporation , deep
percolation as 2 mm per day, the entire rainfall at 80% probability as effective rainfall, and
water conveyance efficiency from the intake of the irrigation system to the delivery at the
farm as 45% in the wet season and 50% in the dry season. A typical calculation of the
paddy area, which can be irrigated in the wet and dry season at Tumauini NIS, is given in
Appendix 1.
4.    The results of the study (Appendix 2) are summarized below for each NIS.
Magapit (service area 10.914 ha): Although the major improvement work pertains
to excavation of drains to relieve the flooding during the wet season, low areas extending
to around 3,600 ha will still be water logged where farmers are not likely to cultivate.
Accordingly, wet season irrigation will be possible only in 7,314 ha. Dry season irrigation
is anticipated in the entire 10,914 ha. The NIS pumps water from the Cagayan river which



- 191 -                          ANNEX 15
Page 2 of 4
has sufficient flow year round. The intake is located sufficiently far from the river mouth
at sea to avoid intrusion of salinity. Sufficient pumping capacity exists.
San Pablo Cabagan (service area 1.273 ha): Besides the main source, there is one
creek having sufficient flow. Water from this creek is diverted to the main canal. Areas
estimated from creek flow (250 ha in wet and 200 ha in dry) are approximate. There
seems to be a good probability of irrigating the full area in the wet season, but in the dry
season about 100 ha would probably not get water.
Tumauini (service area 3,650 ha): There are seven creeks in addition to Tuamauini
river, which provide limnited water for irrigation. An approximation of the areas irrigated
from the creeks during wet season is: Antagan about 300 ha, Malamag 100 ha, Parenza 70
ha, Totto 100 ha, Caronan upper 100 ha and lower 200 ha, Angeapar 50 ha, and Callagan
80 ha. Except for the last from which water is diverted to the main canal network, the
others have check structures irrigating captive areas. The possible area from the creeks in
the wet season is thus 1,000 ha, and only half of this area in the dry season. The available
water remains short throughout the year and irrigation will be possible in only part of the
service area in both the seasons.
Magat District II (service area 23.187 ha): NIA examined in detail the water
availability for irrigation of the entire Magat service area. Water is available for full
service area irrigation in both the seasons. Moreover, the proposed additional area over
the average actual irrigation (1,598 ha in wet and 1,972 ha in dry) includes about 900 ha
which is proposed to be brought under irrigation from check structures on the drains.
Angat-Maasim (service area 31.485 ha!: The main source of water is the regulated
flow from the Angat dam, which is diverted into the canals on both banks at Bustos. The
additional sources are the flow from the uncontrolled catchment of the Angat river
between the Angat dam and the Bustos diversion structure, and the natural flows in the
Maasim river. The water availability and possible irrigation areas have been worked out as
follows:
a)    meeting the requirement of 2,110 ha (service area of two canals from the
upper Maasim diversion structure) from the available Maasim flow, and making up
shortages from the proposed link canal to Maasim river from Bustos system;
b)    meeting the requirement of 1,059 ha (service area of the canal from lower
Maasim diversion structure) from the surplus from (a) above and diverted flow from
Bustos system through the existing link canal; and
c)    meeting the requirement of (a) and (b) above (through the link canals) and
the remaining proposed area (24,000 ha in the wet season and 25,800 ha in the dry
season) from the flows arriving at Bustos dam.



-. 192 -                          ANNEX 15
Page 3 of 4
Should the Angat dam releases for irrigation continue at the past levels, NIA's assessment
shows that there would be enough water to irrigate most of the service area. However,
the analysis does not allow for the obligatory releases downstream of Bustos for the river
channel protection for environmental reasons, and the results are, therefore, tentative.
During the wet season, a large area gets inundated due to higher water level of the
Candappa swamp, which would need very heavy expenditure for protection (not included
in the project). About 7,000 ha of the service area is, therefore, not likely to be under
irrigated or any cultivation in the wet season. The proposed irrigation of 24,500 ha in the
wet season and 28,900 ha in the dry season will be refined further during the detailed
preparation of the improvement proposals.
Camiling (service area 8.580 ha): The NIS gets additional supplies from two small
streams, Bulsa and Cayaling. In the wet season, about 1,400 ha remains water-logged. In
the dry season, water is not sufficient. In both the seasons, therefore, irrigated area is less
than the service area.
Balanac (service area 1,300 ha): The NIS utilises water from two creeks in
addition to Balanac river. Water availability (approximate for creeks) shows that full area
could be irrigated in the wet season. However, due to submergence from Laguna de Bay,
about 100 ha may be too water-logged for crops. During the dry season, only 1,000 ha
could probably be irrigated with the available water.
Baco-Bucayao (service area 6.678 ha): There is sufficient water to irrigate the
entire service area in both seasons.
Pagbahan (service area 1.356 ha): There is sufficient water to irrigate the entire
service area in both seasons.
Jalaur (service area 8.862 ha): The main river has low flow in the dry season. It is
reported that diversion of water upstream of the NIS intake is increasing. Water is short
in the dry season. It is likely that water is being distributed thinly. No increase in dry
season irrigation is, therefore, possible. In the wet season, water is sufficient but about
862 ha remains water-logged. The proposed dry season irrigated area would be refined by
NIA further during the preparation of detailed improvement proposals.
Sibalom-Tigbauan (service area 2,100 ha): The available water is insufficient
during both seasons to irrigate the full service area.
Dipolo (service area 1.465 ha): The NIS utilises the flow in three creeks in
addition to the main river. The NIS actually comprises four independent systems with
diversion structures at each source. The availability of water in the dry season is
insufficient to meet the requirements of the entire service area.
Andanan (service area 5.500 ha): The NIS, with the Andanan river as the source,
is short of water, and transfer of water from the adjacent Wawa river is proposed. The



- 193 -
ANNEX IS
Page 4 of 4
trans-basin canal is also to irrigate 500 ha. Even with transfer of water from Wawa river,
water will be short for the dry season irrigation. The entire 5,500 ha could be irrigated in
the wet season.
M'Lang (service area 3.120 ha): The NIS utilises the flow in the Bulong and Lika
creeks. The available water is short in the dry season, and only about 2,000 ha could be
irrigated.
