Annex 19
Page 1 of 15
PHILIPPINES
WATER RESOURCES DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
1.    This Annex explains the underlying assumptions and the methodology used in the
derivation of economic costs and benefits of the project, and the results of the economic analysis.
The economic rate of return of the project is calculated for the system improvement and repair
component only, since the benefits of other compnents cannot be quantified with any precision.
An analysis of the technical assumptions (e.g., water use efficiency) used in computing project
benefits with and without the project is also presented.
General
2.    The Philippines has become an increasingly open economy in recent years with trade,
remittances, and private capital inflows each expanding rapidly, the last stimulated by the capital
account liberalization of 1992. Financial prices are fairly close to economic prices. This is true
for the exchange rate, interest rate and tradable commodities in general, reflecting minimal
overall distortion in the economy. Based on the proportion of imports and exports in total trade
and a weighted average of import taxes and export taxes, the Standard Conversion Factor (SCF)
for the Philippines was estimated at 0.83. With the macroeconomic and structural reform agenda
currently underway, the long-term average nominal protection is expected to decrease. It is
assumed, however, that some degree of distortion vis-a-vis market prices in the economy is likely
to continue, and hence a SCF of 0.83 has been used to value non-tradable inputs and outputs.
Agrculture
3.    Agriculture accounts for about one-fourth (22%) of the GDP and almost half (45%) of the
labor force. The slowdown in the growth of the sector after the 1 970s has been attributed to: an
overall downward trend in international commodity prices affecting the country's traditional
export crops, continuous deterioration in the inter-sectoral terms of trade, macroeconomic
constraints, and a series of natural calamities. The sector's problems have been exacerbated by
the revised Local Government Code (1992) which devolved inter alia responsibility for
communal irrigation, tertiary roads, extension, and natural resource management to the local
governement units (LGUs). Due to the inadequate financing and implementation capacity of the
LGUs, the delivery and quality of these services have been seriously affected. However, the
planned level of public agricultural expenditures under the Medium Term Philippine
Development Plan (1993-1998) represents a significant increase over previous years.'
World Bank, Philippines: Public Expenditure Management for Sustained and Equitable Growth, September
1995.



- 208 -
Annex 19
Page 2 of 15
4.    The recent liberalization of the trade regime in the context of the government's structural
adjustment program has seen a modest fall in the effective rate of protection in the agriculture
sector from 5% in 1991 to 3% in 1995. However, exportables continue to be taxed at 5% and the
effective protection rates for importables declined from 48% to 38%2. As a result of the recent
agreement to liberalize trade through the World Trade Organization (WTO), market access will
be further expanded through the dismantling of non-trade barriers and reduction of existing
tariffs. As requested by the government, rice has been exempted from the WTO regulations over
the next ten years, although the minimum access requirement has been imposed.
Project C
5.    Investment costs. The project would provide for the improvement of 14 existing national
irrigation systems (NIS) serving about 110,000 ha., and repairs to eight diversion structures (in
NIS other than the 14 selected for improvement) and strengthening of Masiway dam. Investment
costs would include construction of silt excluders, new regulating and control structures,
resectioning of the canals, repairing damaged structures etc.
6.    Operation and Maintenance Costs. These represent the average (1991-94) O&M costs for
each system and are directly associated with the improvement of the NIS. The O&M activities
include canal maintenance, maintenance of service roads, pumping costs (for Magapit PIS), and
maintenance of control gates and structures. O&M costs are phased in, based on the percent of
incremental area generated in each system after project implementation.
7.    It has been agreed with NIA that the improved laterals of the NIS would be handed over
to the IA for water distribution and maintenance of the lateral and sub-laterals, the responsibility
of NIA being only to supply the required amount of water at the head of the lateral. As under the
IOSP II, the lAs will share the cost of the improvement works.
8.    Paddy is the main crop sown in the service area of all the NIS. Paddy production costs
include cash costs, non-cash costs, and imputed costs. Cash costs include seeds/planting
materials, fertilizer, agrochemicals, hired labor, land tax, interest on crop loans, and (payable in
kind) include seeds/planting materials (non-cash portion), irrigation transportation costs incurred
during the purchase of inputs for the farm. Non-cash costs service fee, lease rental,landlord
share, harvester/thresher share, and hired labor (non-cash portion). Imputed costs include unpaid
family labor and exchange labor (valued at prevailing market wage rates), depreciation, interest
on capital investment, and owned land (rental value). A recent country-wide study3 indicates that
non-cash costs account for 34% of total costs, of which shares of the harvester and thresher form
the bulk (42%). Irrigation service fee (ISF) constitutes only 1 percent of the non-cash costs.
Hired labor accounts for a significant portion of cash costs (32%) and unpaid family and operator
2   World Bank, Philippines: Recent Macroeconomic Developments and Reform Efforts, June 1994.
3   International Irrigation Management Institute,Performance and Financial Viability of Irrigated Agriculture in the
Philippines, C.M Wijayaratna and Ma. Delia M. Valdez, September 1995.



- 209 -
Annex 19
Page 3 of 15
labor constitute 31%  of imputed costs. These  percentages are approximately the same on
average in the project service areas.
9.    ISF Rates. These are expressed in cavans4 of paddy and farmers may pay either in kind
or in the equivalent cash amount based on the official support price (P6.0/kg). ISF rates vary
depending on season and type of scheme, ranging from 2 cavans/ha in the wet season for a
diversion system (Balanac) to 7.5 cavans/ha in the wet and dry season for a pump system
(Magapit).
10.   Labo.  Wages are largely determined by market forces with seasonal and regional
variations. The slow pace of agricultural growth in the 1990s has seen the growth of rural labor
supply exceeding demand, thereby increasing pressures for out-migration. Sustainable
development is also threatened as population pressures on upland and coastal areas have
increased, reflecting the lack of employment opportunities in traditional farm  lands5. The
economic cost of unskilled labor was estimated at 60 percent of the market wage which varies in
each of the 14 NIS. This rate is assumed to reflect the marginal productivity of labor in its
alternative use without the project.
11.   The average wage rate for the 18 NIS is P70/day. The average number of man-days for
paddy cultivation is 61, 77, and 77 for rainfed, irrigated (wet season), and irrigated (dry season),
respectively. Since the project is not so large as to greatly alter the demand for unskilled labor
relative to its supply, the wage rate for skilled and unskilled labor in rural areas is not expected to
change as a result of the proposed project.
12.   Crop yields were assumed to be similar with and without the project. All benefits and
costs were adjusted to reflect prevailing relative economic scarcities of factors of production in
the Philippines according to the following assumptions:
(a)    Project investment costs have been converted to economic costs by removing
transfer payments (i.e. taxes and subsidies) and border priced using the SCF of
0.83. The O&M cost (financial) was converted to economic costs using a specific
conversion factor of 0.78.
(b)   Prices (present and projected) for traded goods (rice and fertilizers) at im6port
parity, are based on the most recent World Bank   price projections  of
October/November 1995 with adjustments for quality, freight, insurance,
handling, local transport and marketing margins, and processing costs.
I cavan = 50 kgs
5   World Bank, Economic Developments and Policy Challenges, November 1995.
6   World Bank, Commodity Markets and the Developing Countries, November 1995.



- 210 -
Annex 19
Page 4 of 15
(c)   The opportunity cost of unskilled labor was estimated at 60% of the prevailing
market wage which varies among the 14 NIS.
(d)   Imported components were valued at the foreign exchange rate of P26 to US$1.
(e)   Prices of all non-traded goods and services were adjusted to border prices by
using the SCF of 0.83.
(f)   The project evaluation period is 30 years.
All costs and benefits are in constant 1995 prices.
project Benefits
13.   The proposed project would contribute to the government goals of poverty alleviation and
improved food security. With a high rural poverty incidence (estimated at over 50% in 1995), the
bulk of the beneficiaries under the project are expected to be poor farmers. The main benefit
would be improved operation and maintenance of the country's irrigation infrastructure which
would in turn increase paddy production, farm  incomes, and employment. The annual
incremental rice production due to the project of about 100,000 tons (65% recovery from paddy)
will result in annual foreign exchange savings of about US$30 million in current prices by the
year 2003.
14.   The quantified economic benefits of the project are the value of the increased agricultural
production net of the increased production costs and project development and O&M costs. The
increased production will result from an increase in the presently irrigated area from 145,000 ha
to 188,000 ha, and an increase in average cropping intensity from 133% to 171% for 12 of the 14
NIS.While yield increases are likely to be higher with improved irrigation under the project, no
such increases have been assumed and the project incremental benefits have been estimated
conservatively on the basis of increased cropping intensities and restoration of irrigated area
only.
15.   For the systems under the major repairs sub-component, the benefits were calculated as
savings in future repairs or savings in O&M, or prevention of loss of irrigated areas and/or
expansion in irrigated area, or a combination of these. The key assumption here is that without
any intervention, repairs would be delayed by five years. A shorter delay would substantially
increase the ERRs.
16.   The non-quantified benefits of the project include: (i) additional income generation
through on-farm and off-farm activities; (ii) better and timely farm operations due to improved
drainage; (iii) improved public health because of control of schistosomiasis; and (iv)
environmental benefits from reduction of siltation and promotion of sustainable farming
practices.



- 211 -
Annex 19
Page 5 of 15
Technical Assumptions in Benefit Computation
Water Availability
17.   Of the 14 schemes ( service area 1 10,000 ha), two draw water from the reservoirs along
with use of the adjacent smaller rivers, and 12 are of the run-of -the river type. Of the latter, two,
Magapit and Baco-Bucayao (17,592 ha), have significantly higher supplies in the parent rivers
than the irrigation requirements in both the wet and dry seasons; eight (35,206 ha) have ample
supplies in the wet season, but are short in the dry; and only two, Andanan and Tumauini (9,150
ha) have shortage in both the seasons, but Andanan would receive additional water under the
project from an adjacent stream.
Cropping Intensity With and Without the Project
18.   The area under irrigated crops is anticipated to increase in both the wet and dry seasons.
The area figures are firm in some cases and tentative in others. Based on current figures, in the
wet season, the increase would be 21,900 ha (52 % of the incremental area), from the following
sources:
(a)   relief of flooding in the relatively lower areas where flooding currently inhibits
cropping or damages occur if the farmers plant the crops;
(b)   repairs to the damages to the irrigation system and remodelling of the canals to
deliver water to those areas whch are currently do not get water due to system
deficiencies; and
(c)   diverting water from the drains within the service area or in close vicinity to feed
those portions of the service area where the irrigation system can not deliver water
at all or shortages occur.
19.  In the wet season, the additional area generated would not be limited by the availability of
water or the variation of water use efficiency, except in Tumauini (Andanan would cease to have
a problem after the improvement under the project). The canals have enough capacity to deliver
water during the wet season even at a lower water use efficiency.
20.   In the dry season, the increase would be 20,600 ha (48 % of the incremental area), from
the following sources:
(a)   availability of more area for the dry season crops in the schemes having ample
supplies of water, due to better drainage;
(b)   improvement of system to deliver water to the areas which are currently not
getting water; and
(c)   diversion of water from the drains.



- 212 -
Annex 19
Page 6 of 15
Only in the dry season does water use efficiency play the role of defining what areas can be
irrigated and that also only for the systems which are short of water in the parent streams.
Water Use Efficiency
21.   Unfortunately sufficient data is not available for calculating the water use efficiency in
different years (wet and dry). The irrigated area is recorded as any area which receives water
(irrespective of the number of times received), and benefitted area is defined as that producing
more than 2 tons/ha (less than the yield of rainfed paddy). Invariably, the volume of water
supplied to the laterals or turnouts is not recorded or is not available to calculate the water use
efficiency by sections. Even for the NIS as a whole, data for the supplies to the intake of the
system is not forthcoming. In a few systems (notably Andanan), water is distributed thinly with
the concurrence of the farmers. The water use efficiency for such a system, if calculated on the
data, would be more than 100 %. The question during project preparation, therefore, arose as to
what water use efficiency has a reasonable probability of achievment. The target of 45 % overall
water use efficiency in the wet season and 50 % in the dry season was considered reasonable (the
same targets were used for the reservoir systems, although the Irrigation System Improvement
Plan to be prepared by NIA suggests examining the possibility of achieving 50% efficiency in
the wet season and 55% in the dry season), due to the following reasons:
(a)   All the systems to be improved have existing arrangements for diverting water
from the drains, which receive the surplus water from the irrigated areas, both
from direct runoff as well as from seepage. The percentages vary. More such
diversion would be provided under the project. Even if the area benefitting from
return flow is of the order of 10 %, the water use efficiency would increase by
5%.
(b)   Evapotranspiration needs of the paddy have been conservatively taken as 1 10 %
of pan evaporation although IRRI recommends 100 %.
(c)   Deep percolation losses are taken at a uniform rate of 2 mm/day for the entire
area, although most of the soils are heavy and are under paddy cultivation for a
long time.
(d)   Possible incremental irrigated areas in the dry season were determined from the
water availability in each of the 10-day period. However, if a shortage of 20 % is
allowed in the critical 1 0-day period, the same incremental area calculated at 50 %
efficiency could be irrigated with a lower efficiency of water use.
Economic Analysis
22.   The project components dealing with the institutional strengthening of the National Water
Resources Board, watershed management, and environmental improvement, have not been
included in the computation of economic rates of return because their benefits are extremely
difficult to quantify.



- 213 -
Annex 19
Page 7 of 15
23.   Based on the technical assumptions presented in paras.18-21, the economic analysis was
carried out on 12 of the 14 NIS under the systems improvement component and the 8 systems
under the repair component which together account for 56% of total project cost. Of the 14 NIS
selected for systems improvement, work on six of the systems is scheduled to begin in the first
year of project implementation (1997), with the others following suit in 1998. Of the 8 structures
selected for repair, five are scheduled to begin in 1997, 2 in 1998, and I in 1999.
24.   An economic rate of return was calculated for each of the 12 NIS under the systems
improvement. The overall ERR is about 26%, ranging from 13% to 67%. Separate ERRs were
also calculated for each of the 8 systems under the repair sub-component. Here, the overall ERR
is about 29% ranging from 16% to 58%. The ERRs for the individual NIS and the Net Present
Values (NPVs) calculated at a discount rate of 10% are provided in Tablel. Table 2 presents the
ERRs for the repair sub-component. The combined systems improvement and repair ERR is
about 27%. The overall benefit and cost streams are shown in Table 3.
25.   A project life of 30 years was assumed in calculating the economic rate of return.
Agricultural development period assumed for all the NIS to attain full development would range
from 3 to 5 years. Physical contingencies and engineering, supervision, and administration
expenses estimated at 10% and 7% of direct investment costs were included in the analysis.

Sensitivity and Risks
26.   Sensitivity analysis was conducted to examine the viability of the project under adverse
conditions. The analysis was carried out separately for the systems improvement sub-component
also. The results of the sensitivity analyses are presented in Table 4. Table 5 present results for
the sensitivity analysis carried out for the combined systems improvement and major repair
components. The results indicate that the ERR is fairly robust for the adverse scenarios
presented. In Table 4, the ERR drops to its lowest (16.5%) for the case of the project benefits
being delayed by 3 years. For the case of a reduction in the incremental irrigated area in the dry
season by 50%, the ERR is still high at 19.6%.
27.   Switching values (SVs) were computed to determine the percentage changes in key
variables required to reduce the project's NPV to zero or the ERR to the opportunity cost of
capital. Two rates of opportunity cost of capital were used (15% and 10%) to calculate switching
values. The results of the tests are summarized in Table 6. The SVs are high in general. Irrigated
cropping intensity is the only variable that is relatively most sensitive. The importance of this
variable is underscored by the fact that the main institutional risk is that of non-cooperation by
the lAs resulting in a reduction in irrigated area in the dry season, which depends largely on
improved water use efficiency unlike the wet seaason where the incremental irrigated area would
increase largely due to drainage improvements.(paras. 18-21).
28.   The switching values required to produce an ERR of 15% require future with-project
yields of 2.54 t/ha and 2.3 t/ha in the wet and dry season, respectively. This would be below
present average rainfed yields of 2.72 t/ha. Since no yield improvements were assumed in
calculating the benefits with the project, this scenario is highly improbable.
29.   The SVs for incremental benefits delay, a very real risk given the potential for delays in
project implementation is 3 years at an ERR of 15% and 7 years at an ERR of 10%. The SV at
15% is relatively low, indicative of a fairly critical variable.
30.   The lowest commodity price forecast within the probability distribution at 70%
confidence interval for rice in the year 2000 (in constant 1995 prices) is US$181/ton. The SV at
10% ERR is $184 which implies a 34% fall in rice prices by the time the project attains full
development - a scenario, while possible, is quite unlikely.

3 1.   Based on the discussion of the technical assumptions in paras. 18-21. and the results of
the sensitivity analyses, it is clear that in summary, the calculation of incremental irrigated areas,
and, therefore, the economic rate of return, is not critically dependent on water use efficiency
targets: water availability is not a significant issue in the wet season and for two systems
(Magapit and Baco-Bucayao) in the dry season. Further, for the reservoir-based systems (Magat
District II and Angat), the 50% water use efficiency target in the dry season should be considered
quite reasonable. The reservoir-based systems. Magapit and Baco-Bucayao would account for
about 72% of the incremental irrigated area in the dry season (14,880 ha out of a total
incremental irrigated area of 20,600 ha in the dry season for the whole project). A reduction in
the water use efficiency targets would, therefore, not have a large impact on the ERR. On the
other hand, since the improved systems are to be handed over to the water users. and an essential
objective of the project is to encourage the water users to improve the management of the
systems, it was considered advisable to retain the targets of 45% in the wet season and 50% in
the dry.
